.355.043.553.043-.04-.216-.08-.39-.119-.605.355.13.671.301.949.474.355.216.67.432.91.69a4.2 4.2 0 0 1 1.778 1.64v-.258C14.742 3.712 11.382.045 7.31 0 3.24-.042-.04 3.537 0 7.98c.04 2.416 1.028 4.573 fill="none" class="linkedin_svg__responsive-icon" viewBox="0 0 48 48"><g clip-path="url(#linkedin_svg__a)"><path fill="currentColor" d="M44.447 0H3.544C1.584 0 0 1.547 0 3.46V44.53C0 46.444 1.584 48 prose"><p>Symantec, a division of <a href="https://software.broadcom.com/">Broadcom Software</a>, has observed threat actors targeting server machines in order to spread the LockBit ransomware threat throughout compromised networks.</p>

<p>In one attack observed by Symantec, LockBit was seen identifying domain-related information, creating a Group Policy for lateral movement, and executing a "gpupdate /force" command on all systems within the same domain, which forcefully updates group policy.</p>

<h2>LockBit</h2>

<p>LockBit is a ransomware-as-a-service (RaaS) operated by malicious actors Symantec tracks as Syrphid.</p>

<p>Shortly after it first appeared in September 2019, the Syrphid gang expanded its operations, using a network of affiliates to deploy the LockBit ransomware on victim networks. The ransomware, which has currently reached <a href="https://www.darkreading.com/threat-intelligence/lockbit-3-debut-bug-bounty-program">version 3.0</a>, has evolved over the past few years, as has its operators who have recently <a href="https://www.bleepingcomputer.com/news/security/lockbit-30-introduces-the-first-ransomware-bug-bounty-program/">launched a bug bounty program</a> in order to weed out weaknesses in the malware’s code and the RaaS operation as a whole.</p>

<h2>Attack chain</h2>

<p>In one observed instance, before dropping and executing the LockBit ransomware, an attacker had RDP access to the enterprise network for a couple of weeks at least. This access may have been obtained through remote desktop applications such as AnyDesk or Windows RDP, or by exploiting a known vulnerability, etc.</p>

<p>LockBit behaves differently on server machines with domain controllers than on Windows 10 machines. When executed on a server, it has the capability to spread through the network using Group Policy. On Windows 10 machines it performs routine ransomware activity and encrypts files.</p>

<p>When LockBit is executed on a server machine it carries out the following actions:</p>

<p>1. Debugger check</p>

<ul>
	<li>LockBit first checks if the malware process is being debugged. If this is the case, it goes into an infinite loop.</li>
</ul>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-image-inline prose"><figure class="paragraph-image-inline__figure"><img alt="Figure 1. If malware process is being debugged, LockBit goes into an infinite loop" loading="lazy" width="1076" height="535" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent" srcSet="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig1.png.webp%3Fitok%3Du_HkYRKp%201x&amp;w=1080&amp;q=75 1x, /_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig1.png.webp%3Fitok%3Du_HkYRKp%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75 2x" src="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig1.png.webp%3Fitok%3Du_HkYRKp%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75"/><figcaption class="paragraph-image-inline__caption">Figure 1. If malware process is being debugged, LockBit goes into an infinite loop</figcaption></figure></div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>2. Language Check</p>

<ul>
	<li>It calls <strong>GetSystemDefaultUILanguage</strong> and <strong>GetUserDefaultUILanguage</strong> to check the language.</li>
	<li>If the language matches with the one on the malware’s list then it terminates immediately.</li>
	<li>LockBit does not target Russia or a selection of nearby countries.</li>
</ul>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-image-inline prose"><figure class="paragraph-image-inline__figure"><img alt="Figure 2. LockBit calls GetSystemDefaultUILanguage and GetUserDefaultUILanguage to check the language." loading="lazy" width="869" height="607" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent" srcSet="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig2.png.webp%3Fitok%3DduNJlqBY%201x&amp;w=1080&amp;q=75 1x, /_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig2.png.webp%3Fitok%3DduNJlqBY%201x&amp;w=1920&amp;q=75 2x" src="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig2.png.webp%3Fitok%3DduNJlqBY%201x&amp;w=1920&amp;q=75"/><figcaption class="paragraph-image-inline__caption">Figure 2. LockBit calls GetSystemDefaultUILanguage and GetUserDefaultUILanguage to check the language.</figcaption></figure></div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>3. End running processes and disable services</p>

<ul>
	<li>LockBit ends a list of running processes related to malware analysis and other processes like Process Explorer, Process Monitor, Wireshark, Dumpcap, Process Hacker, cmd.exe, TeamViewer, Notepad, Notepad++, WordPad etc.</li>
	<li>Disables a list of services related to SQL, backup, and MSExchange etc.</li>
</ul>

<p>4. Privilege escalation</p>

<ul>
	<li>Duplicates the token by calling <strong>DuplicateTokenEx</strong> and creates a new process using<strong> CreateProcessAsUserW.</strong></li>
	<li>After it achieves privilege escalation, LockBit relaunches itself under DLLHost.exe. Once the new process is spawned, the LockBit process ends itself.</li>
</ul>

<p>5. Bypass UAC</p>

<ul>
	<li>LockBit injects code into dllhost.exe with CLSIDs of COM objects, which runs the following command to bypass UAC:</li>
</ul>

<p>A. Exploiting USERENV.dll to bypass UAC</p>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>B. Bypass method in hfiref0x’s UACME</p>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>C. Exploiting the ICMLuaUtil elevated COM Interface-Object</p>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>6. LockBit creates a copy of itself under the SYSVOL directory “c:\windows\sysvol\domain\scripts\&lt; Lockbit executable&gt;”</p>

<p>7. Creating a Group Policy:</p>

<ul>
	<li>Once the malware identifies it is running as an admin user and a domain controller is installed on the system, it creates a Group Policy to stop services, end processes, and copy LockBit etc.</li>
	<li>Under the “<strong>C:\Windows\SYSVOL\domain\Policies\&lt;policy GUID&gt;</strong>” folder, LockBit creates XML files that are required for the Group Policy.</li>
</ul>

<p>Computer configurations:</p>

<ul>
	<li>It first creates a policy to turn off Windows Defender, suppress all notifications, disable file submissions, turn off real-time protection etc.</li>
	<li>It then maps the network drive through Group Policy.</li>
	<li>Disables services related to SQL server at startup.</li>
</ul>

<p>User Configurations:</p>

<ul>
	<li>The malware copied the ransomware from SYSVOL to the Desktop directory.</li>
	<li>It then creates a scheduled task to end the list of processes previously mentioned.</li>
</ul>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-image-inline prose"><figure class="paragraph-image-inline__figure"><img alt="Figure 3.Group Policy XML file used to copy LockBit from the shared SYSVOL location to client’s desktop location." loading="lazy" width="1076" height="415" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent" srcSet="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig3.png.webp%3Fitok%3DEIUYqRAF%201x&amp;w=1080&amp;q=75 1x, /_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig3.png.webp%3Fitok%3DEIUYqRAF%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75 2x" src="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig3.png.webp%3Fitok%3DEIUYqRAF%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75"/><figcaption class="paragraph-image-inline__caption">Figure 3.Group Policy XML file used to copy LockBit from the shared SYSVOL location to client’s desktop location.</figcaption></figure></div><div class="paragraph paragraph-image-inline prose"><figure class="paragraph-image-inline__figure"><img alt="Figure 4. Group Policy created by LockBit can be seen in the Group Policy Management console." loading="lazy" width="962" height="768" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent" srcSet="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig4.png.webp%3Fitok%3Deat13Pfg%201x&amp;w=1080&amp;q=75 1x, /_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig4.png.webp%3Fitok%3Deat13Pfg%201x&amp;w=2048&amp;q=75 2x" src="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig4.png.webp%3Fitok%3Deat13Pfg%201x&amp;w=2048&amp;q=75"/><figcaption class="paragraph-image-inline__caption">Figure 4. Group Policy created by LockBit can be seen in the Group Policy Management console.</figcaption></figure></div><div class="paragraph paragraph-image-inline prose"><figure class="paragraph-image-inline__figure"><img alt="Figure 5. Group Policy details to disable Defender and several additional options." loading="lazy" width="1076" height="691" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent" srcSet="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig5.png.webp%3Fitok%3DLIGJNByW%201x&amp;w=1080&amp;q=75 1x, /_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig5.png.webp%3Fitok%3DLIGJNByW%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75 2x" src="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig5.png.webp%3Fitok%3DLIGJNByW%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75"/><figcaption class="paragraph-image-inline__caption">Figure 5. Group Policy details to disable Defender and several additional options.</figcaption></figure></div><div class="paragraph paragraph-image-inline prose"><figure class="paragraph-image-inline__figure"><img alt="Figure 6. Group Policy used to map network drives." loading="lazy" width="1076" height="716" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent" srcSet="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig6.png.webp%3Fitok%3DSQiWMXKf%201x&amp;w=1080&amp;q=75 1x, /_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig6.png.webp%3Fitok%3DSQiWMXKf%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75 2x" src="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig6.png.webp%3Fitok%3DSQiWMXKf%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75"/><figcaption class="paragraph-image-inline__caption">Figure 6. Group Policy used to map network drives.</figcaption></figure></div><div class="paragraph paragraph-image-inline prose"><figure class="paragraph-image-inline__figure"><img alt="Figure 7. Group Policy used to disable SQL services at startup." loading="lazy" width="1076" height="622" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent" srcSet="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig7.png.webp%3Fitok%3DM8DYeKri%201x&amp;w=1080&amp;q=75 1x, /_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig7.png.webp%3Fitok%3DM8DYeKri%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75 2x" src="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig7.png.webp%3Fitok%3DM8DYeKri%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75"/><figcaption class="paragraph-image-inline__caption">Figure 7. Group Policy used to disable SQL services at startup.</figcaption></figure></div><div class="paragraph paragraph-image-inline prose"><figure class="paragraph-image-inline__figure"><img alt="Figure 8 Group Policy used to copy LockBit from the SYSVOL shared location to the desktop." loading="lazy" width="1076" height="622" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent" srcSet="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig8.png.webp%3Fitok%3DgnGDclkz%201x&amp;w=1080&amp;q=75 1x, /_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig8.png.webp%3Fitok%3DgnGDclkz%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75 2x" src="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig8.png.webp%3Fitok%3DgnGDclkz%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75"/><figcaption class="paragraph-image-inline__caption">Figure 8 Group Policy used to copy LockBit from the SYSVOL shared location to the desktop.</figcaption></figure></div><div class="paragraph paragraph-image-inline prose"><figure class="paragraph-image-inline__figure"><img alt="Figure 9. Group Policy used to end processes using the taskkill command." loading="lazy" width="1076" height="904" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent" srcSet="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig9.png.webp%3Fitok%3D2uEaTzwZ%201x&amp;w=1080&amp;q=75 1x, /_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig9.png.webp%3Fitok%3D2uEaTzwZ%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75 2x" src="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig9.png.webp%3Fitok%3D2uEaTzwZ%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75"/><figcaption class="paragraph-image-inline__caption">Figure 9. Group Policy used to end processes using the taskkill command.</figcaption></figure></div><div class="paragraph paragraph-image-inline prose"><figure class="paragraph-image-inline__figure"><img alt="Figure 10. Group Policy used to execute the LockBit ransomware." loading="lazy" width="1076" height="904" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent" srcSet="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig10.png.webp%3Fitok%3DrgA9NN2J%201x&amp;w=1080&amp;q=75 1x, /_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig10.png.webp%3Fitok%3DrgA9NN2J%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75 2x" src="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig10.png.webp%3Fitok%3DrgA9NN2J%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75"/><figcaption class="paragraph-image-inline__caption">Figure 10. Group Policy used to execute the LockBit ransomware.</figcaption></figure></div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>8. Lateral movement:&nbsp;</p>

<ul>
	<li>LockBit launches powershell.exe to run the command shown below in order to search through all the computers on the Active Directory. For each host it uses the GPUpdate force command (gpupdate) to apply the newly created Group Policy.</li>
</ul>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>9. Executes gpupdate command on the domain controller where LockBit is running. Also runs gpupdate to run policies from the computer configurations and user configurations.</p>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>10. Firewall</p>

<ul>
	<li>LockBit reads firewall rules using the Windows Defender Firewall with Advanced Security API's “<strong>FwPolicy2</strong>” object. The following CLSID COM object is called:</li>
</ul>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>11. Impact</p>

<ul>
	<li>LockBit attempts to delete shadow copies using&nbsp;VSSADMIN&nbsp;and&nbsp;WMIC<strong>.</strong>&nbsp;It also tries to disable recovery using&nbsp;the BCDEdit&nbsp;command.</li>
</ul>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><ul>
	<li>Deletes Windows event logs using:</li>
</ul>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>12. Encrypts files and appends the&nbsp;.lockbit&nbsp;file extension.</p>

<p>13. MSHTA.exe</p>

<ul>
	<li>Creates the file lockbit.hta and executes it to display a ransom note.</li>
</ul>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-image-inline prose"><figure class="paragraph-image-inline__figure"><img alt="Figure 11. LockBit ransom note." loading="lazy" width="1076" height="566" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent" srcSet="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig11.png.webp%3Fitok%3DsckkZjNn%201x&amp;w=1080&amp;q=75 1x, /_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig11.png.webp%3Fitok%3DsckkZjNn%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75 2x" src="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig11.png.webp%3Fitok%3DsckkZjNn%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75"/><figcaption class="paragraph-image-inline__caption">Figure 11. LockBit ransom note.</figcaption></figure></div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><p>LockBit has been one of, if not the most active of RaaS gangs in 2022. The drop in Conti activity in May helped LockBit reach the top spot, with some reports stating that the threat was behind as much as <a href="https://www.darkreading.com/threat-intelligence/without-conti-on-the-scene-ransomware-attacks-fell-in-may">40% of ransomware attacks</a>.</p>

<p>LockBit’s success is also due to its developers and affiliates continued evolution of features and tactics, which include the malware’s <a href="https://www.splunk.com/en_us/blog/security/gone-in-52-seconds-and-42-minutes-a-comparative-analysis-of-ransomware-encryption-speed.html">fast encryption speed</a>, ability to target both Windows and <a href="https://www.bleepingcomputer.com/news/security/linux-version-of-lockbit-ransomware-targets-vmware-esxi-servers/">Linux machines</a>, its <a href="https://www.bleepingcomputer.com/news/security/lockbit-ransomware-recruiting-insiders-to-breach-corporate-networks/">brash recruitment drives</a>, and <a href="https://therecord.media/rio-de-janeiro-finance-department-hit-with-lockbit-ransomware/">high-profile targets</a>. In addition, as previously mentioned, the launch of a rewards program for vulnerabilities in LockBit’s code and for suggestions on improving the RaaS operation will no-doubt help the ransomware remain a serious threat to organizations.</p>

<h2>Indicators of Compromise (IOCs)</h2>

<p>If an IOC is malicious and the file available to us, Symantec Endpoint products will detect and block that file.</p>

<ul>
	<li>5181d2e71e8e73a82712a483a80aaea94e1efa785f2b8b8ee9641544c0b652f0 - lockbit_6341d6e5844c8289.exe</li>
	<li>llll.exe – copy of LockBit ransomware</li>
	<li>hxxps://temp[.]sh/AErDa/LockBit_6341D6E5844C8289[.]exe - Payload URL</li>
</ul>

<h2>MITRE Techniques</h2>
</div><div class="paragraph paragraph-image-inline prose"><figure class="paragraph-image-inline__figure"><img alt="Figure 12. MITRE techniques used by the LockBit ransomware." loading="lazy" width="1076" height="591" decoding="async" data-nimg="1" style="color:transparent" srcSet="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig12.png.webp%3Fitok%3DR28pOpl_%201x&amp;w=1080&amp;q=75 1x, /_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig12.png.webp%3Fitok%3DR28pOpl_%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75 2x" src="/_next/image?url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.security.com%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Fstyles%2Fblogs_inline_medium%2Fpublic%2F2022-07%2FFig12.png.webp%3Fitok%3DR28pOpl_%201x&amp;w=3840&amp;q=75"/><figcaption class="paragraph-image-inline__caption">Figure 12. MITRE techniques used by the LockBit ransomware.</figcaption></figure></div><div class="paragraph paragraph-rich-text prose"><h2>Protection/Mitigation</h2>

<p>For the latest protection updates, please visit the <a href="https://www.broadcom.com/support/security-center/protection-bulletin">Symantec Protection Bulletin</a>.</p>

<p>Symantec Endpoint Protection (SEP) protects against ransomware attacks using multiple static and dynamic technologies.</p>

<p><strong>AV Protection</strong></p>

<ul>
	<li>Ransom.LockBit</li>
	<li>Ransom.LockBit!g2</li>
	<li>Scr.Malscript!gen1</li>
</ul>

<p><strong>Behavior Protection</strong></p>

<ul>
	<li>SONAR.RansomLckbit!g3</li>
	<li>SONAR.RansomNokibi!g1</li>
	<li>SONAR.RansomLckbit!g1</li>
</ul>

<p><strong>Intrusion Prevention System (IPS) Protection</strong></p>

<ul>
	<li>[SID: 33705] Attack: Lockbit Ransomware Binary Copy GPO Config</li>
	<li>[SID: 33706] Attack: Lockbit Ransomware Services Disable GPO Config</li>
	<li>[SID: 33707] Attack: Lockbit Ransomware Enable Share GPO Config</li>
	<li>[SID: 33708] Attack: Lockbit Ransomware Security Services Taskkill GPO</li>
</ul>

<p>Symantec Data Center Security (DCS) hardening policies for Windows Servers and Domain Controllers prevent LockBit ransomware installation. The default DCS lockdown prevents lateral movement of LockBit ransomware on the network and protects servers from LockBit execution attempts to tamper with Group Policies and critical system resources.</p>

Bulletin\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eSymantec Endpoint Protection (SEP) protects against ransomware attacks using multiple static and dynamic technologies.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAV (DCS) hardening policies for Windows Servers and Domain Controllers prevent LockBit ransomware installation. The default DCS lockdown prevents lateral movement of LockBit ransomware on the network and protects servers from LockBit execution attempts to tamper with Group Policies and critical system resources.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"15:T4672,"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"\u003cp\u003eChinese APT group Jewelbug (aka REF7707, CL-STA-0049, Earth Alux) has been highly active in recent months, targeting organizations in South America, South Asia, Taiwan and Russia. One of its intrusions was on the network of a Russian IT service provider and lasted for the first five months of 2025.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAttackers had access to code repository and software build systems that they could potentially leverage to carry out supply chain attacks targeting the company’s customers in Russia. Notably too, the attackers were exfiltrating data to Yandex Cloud. Yandex is a popular service in Russia, so the attackers likely chose to use it in order to avoid raising suspicions.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn other activity on a large South American government organization in July 2025, Jewelbug deployed a new backdoor that appears to be under development by the group.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug also compromised the network of a Taiwanese company, as well as another IT provider based in South Asia in recent months. However, its targeting of a Russian company is of particular note as Chinese and Russian threat actors have, until recently, rarely been seen to be attacking each other. Jewelbug’s attack is the continuation of a trend that seems to have begun following Russia’s invasion of Ukraine.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttack on Russian IT service provider\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe first suspicious activity that occurred on this network was the appearance of a file named 7zup.exe (Command line: \u003cem\u003eCSIDL_PROFILE\\public\\downloads\\7zup.exe -d -remote up\u003c/em\u003e), which is a renamed copy of cdb.exe, a benign Microsoft signed binary. CDB is the Microsoft Console Debugger. Use of a renamed version of cbd.exe is a hallmark of Jewelbug activity. CDB can be used to run shellcode and bypass application whitelisting. It can also be used to launch executables, run DLLs and terminate security solutions, making it a powerful tool. Microsoft recommends that CDB should be blocked from running by default and whitelisted for specific users only when it’s explicitly needed.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOther activity on this network included credential dumping, and persistence and elevation of privileges via scheduled tasks (schtasks). The attackers also attempted to cover up their activity by clearing Windows Event Logs.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe use of Yandex Cloud to exfiltrate data was also a probable attempt by the attackers to remain under the radar as Yandex is a legitimate and commonly used cloud service in Russia. For this reason, it is unlikely to be blocked by Russian enterprises, and its use is less likely to raise suspicions. To exfiltrate the victims’ data the attackers used a malicious sample that they had named “yandex2.exe.”\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAs mentioned previously, the attackers were also targeting machines with build systems and code repository systems, potentially seeking to leverage access to the source code to carry out a supply chain attack targeting the company’s customers in Russia. IT service providers are popular targets for attackers seeking to carry out supply chain attacks as they often have extensive access to their customers’ systems and may be able to automatically deploy updates or software across a large number of networks simultaneously, potentially giving the attackers access to, or allowing them to infect, a huge number of organizations at the same time.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt appears attackers may have been on this network for some time, with the first indication of suspicious activity dating from January 2025, while the most recent suspicious activity on this network occurred in May 2025.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eNew backdoor deployed in South American victim\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug also compromised a network belonging to a South American government department. It appears Jewelbug has been on the network of the department multiple times, or else has maintained persistence on the network for a long time, because suspicious activity was first seen in this organization in September 2024, with the most recent activity seen in July 2025.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn the September 2024 activity, the attackers attempted to add a new user to the network, as well as attempting to deploy a remote access tool to gain access to machines. They also used the legitimate AnyDesk remote management software, and deployed the 7-zip archive manager, which is often used to pack files before they are exfiltrated from victim machines. In the more recent activity in July 2025, the attackers used a legitimate executable for DLL sideloading, and the SMBExec tool for likely lateral movement on the network. They also used scheduled tasks for persistence, and BITSAdmin and the curl tool, probably to exfiltrate data.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAlso in this organization, Jewelbug deployed what appears to be a new backdoor in development by the group.\u0026nbsp;This malware leverages Microsoft Graph API and OneDrive as its command and control (C\u0026amp;C) servers. The malware deployed on the victim network appears to have some issues and limitations, perhaps pointing to it being a work in progress, or some inexperience on the part of the developer. However, activity we do see being carried out by this malware includes:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eObtains a list of files from targeted machines and uploads this to OneDrive\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eDoes some logging into C:\\ProgramData\\application.ini. Some examples of this logging includes:\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003einit successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eFreeFileInformation Fuc successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eC:\\Users\\Public\\Libraries~\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCreateDirectory\u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp;Successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eGet Token successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCreate Folder In OneDrive successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eHttpSendRequestWPtr Error Code:0\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCreates the directory: C:\\Users\\Public\\Libraries~ and hides it on the victim machine\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe malware also obtains the infected machine’s IP, Windows version, and hostname. In some cases, it also collects the machine identifier. It uploads this information to OneDrive.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe logging done by this malware is of note as it points to this malware possibly being “tested” by the attackers. It is also notable as it shows that Jewelbug is continuing to develop new malware. The\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.security.com/threat-intelligence/cloud-espionage-attacks\"\u003euse of Microsoft Graph API\u003c/a\u003e and OneDrive for C\u0026amp;C by Jewelbug is also interesting as it minimizes malicious indicators that would be observable to traditional security software, making this activity much harder to detect.\u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eBYOVD technique used to target Taiwanese software company\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug was also on the network of a Taiwanese software company in October and November 2024. Unlike the later activity targeting the Russian IT service provider, however, there was no evidence of a software supply chain attack motivation. The attackers used DLL sideloading to load malware payloads. DLL sideloading is a very popular tactic among Chinese threat groups. ShadowPad was also deployed on this network. ShadowPad is a powerful modular backdoor that is exclusively used by Chinese threat actors. The attackers also used the KillAV tool to disable security software, as well as deploying a publicly available tool called EchoDrv, which permits abuse of the Kernel read/write vulnerability in the ECHOAC anti-cheat driver. This is likely an example of the attackers using the bring-your-own-vulnerable-driver (BYOVD) technique as an attempt to avoid their malicious activity being detected. They also created scheduled tasks for persistence.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe attackers leveraged LSASS and Mimikatz for dumping credentials, and Fast Reverse Proxy, which can expose local servers to the public internet, while they also deployed several publicly available tools for discovery and privilege escalation. The tools (PrintNotifyPotato, Coerced Potato, and Sweet Potato) are all freely available on GitHub. The attackers also used a publicly available tunnelling tool called Earthworm in a likely attempt to mask commands or data being sent to and from the victim network. The attackers were on this network for approximately three weeks, indicating some skill at remaining under the radar.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOn the networks of both the Taiwanese software company and the South American government agency, a renamed version of the Microsoft Console Debugger (cdb.exe) tool was used. Jewelbug’s use of this tool is notable as it is a relatively under the radar tool, despite it having numerous uses that could be beneficial to malicious actors, as discussed above. In one instance we also saw the CDB tool being injected into mspaint.exe. Mspaint has \u003ca href=\"https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/advanced-backdoor-squidoor/\"\u003epreviously been documented\u003c/a\u003e as being used by Jewelbug to inject malware.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug’s preference for using cloud services and other legitimate tools in its operations indicates that remaining under the radar and establishing a stealthy and persistent presence on victim networks is of utmost importance to this group.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003ePrevious Jewelbug activity\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug is believed to have been active since mid-2023, which was when binaries associated with it were first uploaded to VirusTotal. As well as the attacks mentioned in this blog, Jewelbug has also \u003ca href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security-labs/fragile-web-ref7707\"\u003ebeen associated\u003c/a\u003e with attacks targeting organizations in Southeast Asia, including a university, telecoms organization and a government ministry. Up to this point the group has been associated only with attacks on organizations in Asia and South America, there has been no previous documentation of the group targeting organizations in Russia. A \u003ca href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security-labs/fragile-web-ref7707\"\u003eblog released by security company Elastic earlier this year\u003c/a\u003e did say it had seen Jewelbug attackers using a domain that mimicked a Russian organization, but they did not identify any victims in Russia as part of that activity.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat blog also detailed Jewelbug’s use of unique malware called Finaldraft, Pathloader and Guidloader. Finaldraft is a full-featured remote administration tool with the ability to accept add-on modules that extend functionality. It also:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eHas support for proxying network traffic.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eUses third-party Microsoft Graph API for C\u0026amp;C.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eSupports both Windows and Linux versions.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003ePathloader and Guidloader are malware used to download and execute encrypted shellcode in memory. They have only been observed in association with Finaldraft.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/advanced-backdoor-squidoor/\"\u003ePalo Alto also published a report\u003c/a\u003e about the custom malware used by Jewelbug, though it dubbed the Finaldraft backdoor Squidoor. It also noted that Jewelbug gained access to networks of interest by exploiting various vulnerabilities in Internet Information Services (IIS) servers before deploying webshells on infected servers. It also said that as well as using the Microsoft Graph API for C\u0026amp;C communication, the attackers also used DNS and ICMP tunnelling.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003ePrevious reporting about Jewelbug has also noted the use of the CDB tool, the use of mspaint.exe to inject commands, and the wide-ranging use of dual-use and living off the land tools by the attackers as well, all of which was also seen in the recent activity observed by the Threat Hunter Team.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAll indications point to Jewelbug being of Chinese origin, with its motivation most likely to be espionage and maintaining a long-term and stealthy presence on compromised networks.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eChinese threat actors looking in a new direction?\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe most notable element of all this recent Jewelbug activity is the targeting of a Russian IT service provider by the Chinese APT group. When it comes to things like the Russia-Ukraine conflict and other geopolitical matters, China has traditionally backed, or at least not opposed, Russia, with the two considered to be loosely allied. The targeting of a Russian organization by a Chinese APT group shows, however, that Russia is not out-of-bounds when it comes to operations by China-based actors. The fact that there are indications the IT service provider may have been targeted for the purposes of a software supply chain attack on the company’s customers in Russia is also notable as it means this attack had the potential to give the attackers access to a large number of companies in the country, which they could have used for cyber espionage or disruption.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug’s use of a new backdoor-in-development in this set of activity is also important as it shows that the group is continuing to actively develop its toolset and capabilities. Jewelbug, as a relatively new Chinese APT group, is one to watch as it has the skills to develop its own malware and maintain a long-term and stealthy presence on networks.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eIndicators of compromise (IOCs)\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFile Earth Alux) has been highly active in recent months, targeting organizations in South America, South Asia, Taiwan and Russia. One of its intrusions was on the network of a Russian IT service provider and lasted for the first five months of 2025.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAttackers had access to code repository and software build systems that they could potentially leverage to carry out supply chain attacks targeting the company’s customers in Russia. Notably too, the attackers were exfiltrating data to Yandex Cloud. Yandex is a popular service in Russia, so the attackers likely chose to use it in order to avoid raising suspicions.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn other activity on a large South American government organization in July 2025, Jewelbug deployed a new backdoor that appears to be under development by the group.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug also compromised the network of a Taiwanese company, as well as another IT provider based in South Asia in recent months. However, its targeting of a Russian company is of particular note as Chinese and Russian threat actors have, until recently, rarely been seen to be attacking each other. Jewelbug’s attack is the continuation of a trend that seems to have begun following Russia’s invasion of Ukraine.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttack on Russian IT service provider\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe first suspicious activity that occurred on this network was the appearance of a file named 7zup.exe (Command line: \u003cem\u003eCSIDL_PROFILE\\public\\downloads\\7zup.exe -d -remote up\u003c/em\u003e), which is a renamed copy of cdb.exe, a benign Microsoft signed binary. CDB is the Microsoft Console Debugger. Use of a renamed version of cbd.exe is a hallmark of Jewelbug activity. CDB can be used to run shellcode and bypass application whitelisting. It can also be used to launch executables, run DLLs and terminate security solutions, making it a powerful tool. Microsoft recommends that CDB should be blocked from running by default and whitelisted for specific users only when it’s explicitly needed.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOther activity on this network included credential dumping, and persistence and elevation of privileges via scheduled tasks (schtasks). The attackers also attempted to cover up their activity by clearing Windows Event Logs.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe use of Yandex Cloud to exfiltrate data was also a probable attempt by the attackers to remain under the radar as Yandex is a legitimate and commonly used cloud service in Russia. For this reason, it is unlikely to be blocked by Russian enterprises, and its use is less likely to raise suspicions. To exfiltrate the victims’ data the attackers used a malicious sample that they had named “yandex2.exe.”\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAs mentioned previously, the attackers were also targeting machines with build systems and code repository systems, potentially seeking to leverage access to the source code to carry out a supply chain attack targeting the company’s customers in Russia. IT service providers are popular targets for attackers seeking to carry out supply chain attacks as they often have extensive access to their customers’ systems and may be able to automatically deploy updates or software across a large number of networks simultaneously, potentially giving the attackers access to, or allowing them to infect, a huge number of organizations at the same time.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIt appears attackers may have been on this network for some time, with the first indication of suspicious activity dating from January 2025, while the most recent suspicious activity on this network occurred in May 2025.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eNew backdoor deployed in South American victim\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug also compromised a network belonging to a South American government department. It appears Jewelbug has been on the network of the department multiple times, or else has maintained persistence on the network for a long time, because suspicious activity was first seen in this organization in September 2024, with the most recent activity seen in July 2025.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn the September 2024 activity, the attackers attempted to add a new user to the network, as well as attempting to deploy a remote access tool to gain access to machines. They also used the legitimate AnyDesk remote management software, and deployed the 7-zip archive manager, which is often used to pack files before they are exfiltrated from victim machines. In the more recent activity in July 2025, the attackers used a legitimate executable for DLL sideloading, and the SMBExec tool for likely lateral movement on the network. They also used scheduled tasks for persistence, and BITSAdmin and the curl tool, probably to exfiltrate data.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAlso in this organization, Jewelbug deployed what appears to be a new backdoor in development by the group.\u0026nbsp;This malware leverages Microsoft Graph API and OneDrive as its command and control (C\u0026amp;C) servers. The malware deployed on the victim network appears to have some issues and limitations, perhaps pointing to it being a work in progress, or some inexperience on the part of the developer. However, activity we do see being carried out by this malware includes:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eObtains a list of files from targeted machines and uploads this to OneDrive\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eDoes some logging into C:\\ProgramData\\application.ini. Some examples of this logging includes:\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003einit successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eFreeFileInformation Fuc successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eC:\\Users\\Public\\Libraries~\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCreateDirectory\u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp;Successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eGet Token successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCreate Folder In OneDrive successfully!\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eHttpSendRequestWPtr Error Code:0\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eCreates the directory: C:\\Users\\Public\\Libraries~ and hides it on the victim machine\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eThe malware also obtains the infected machine’s IP, Windows version, and hostname. In some cases, it also collects the machine identifier. It uploads this information to OneDrive.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe logging done by this malware is of note as it points to this malware possibly being “tested” by the attackers. It is also notable as it shows that Jewelbug is continuing to develop new malware. The\u0026nbsp;\u003ca href=\"https://www.security.com/threat-intelligence/cloud-espionage-attacks\"\u003euse of Microsoft Graph API\u003c/a\u003e and OneDrive for C\u0026amp;C by Jewelbug is also interesting as it minimizes malicious indicators that would be observable to traditional security software, making this activity much harder to detect.\u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp; \u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eBYOVD technique used to target Taiwanese software company\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug was also on the network of a Taiwanese software company in October and November 2024. Unlike the later activity targeting the Russian IT service provider, however, there was no evidence of a software supply chain attack motivation. The attackers used DLL sideloading to load malware payloads. DLL sideloading is a very popular tactic among Chinese threat groups. ShadowPad was also deployed on this network. ShadowPad is a powerful modular backdoor that is exclusively used by Chinese threat actors. The attackers also used the KillAV tool to disable security software, as well as deploying a publicly available tool called EchoDrv, which permits abuse of the Kernel read/write vulnerability in the ECHOAC anti-cheat driver. This is likely an example of the attackers using the bring-your-own-vulnerable-driver (BYOVD) technique as an attempt to avoid their malicious activity being detected. They also created scheduled tasks for persistence.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe attackers leveraged LSASS and Mimikatz for dumping credentials, and Fast Reverse Proxy, which can expose local servers to the public internet, while they also deployed several publicly available tools for discovery and privilege escalation. The tools (PrintNotifyPotato, Coerced Potato, and Sweet Potato) are all freely available on GitHub. The attackers also used a publicly available tunnelling tool called Earthworm in a likely attempt to mask commands or data being sent to and from the victim network. The attackers were on this network for approximately three weeks, indicating some skill at remaining under the radar.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOn the networks of both the Taiwanese software company and the South American government agency, a renamed version of the Microsoft Console Debugger (cdb.exe) tool was used. Jewelbug’s use of this tool is notable as it is a relatively under the radar tool, despite it having numerous uses that could be beneficial to malicious actors, as discussed above. In one instance we also saw the CDB tool being injected into mspaint.exe. Mspaint has \u003ca href=\"https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/advanced-backdoor-squidoor/\"\u003epreviously been documented\u003c/a\u003e as being used by Jewelbug to inject malware.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug’s preference for using cloud services and other legitimate tools in its operations indicates that remaining under the radar and establishing a stealthy and persistent presence on victim networks is of utmost importance to this group.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003ePrevious Jewelbug activity\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug is believed to have been active since mid-2023, which was when binaries associated with it were first uploaded to VirusTotal. As well as the attacks mentioned in this blog, Jewelbug has also \u003ca href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security-labs/fragile-web-ref7707\"\u003ebeen associated\u003c/a\u003e with attacks targeting organizations in Southeast Asia, including a university, telecoms organization and a government ministry. Up to this point the group has been associated only with attacks on organizations in Asia and South America, there has been no previous documentation of the group targeting organizations in Russia. A \u003ca href=\"https://www.elastic.co/security-labs/fragile-web-ref7707\"\u003eblog released by security company Elastic earlier this year\u003c/a\u003e did say it had seen Jewelbug attackers using a domain that mimicked a Russian organization, but they did not identify any victims in Russia as part of that activity.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThat blog also detailed Jewelbug’s use of unique malware called Finaldraft, Pathloader and Guidloader. Finaldraft is a full-featured remote administration tool with the ability to accept add-on modules that extend functionality. It also:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003eHas support for proxying network traffic.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eUses third-party Microsoft Graph API for C\u0026amp;C.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003eSupports both Windows and Linux versions.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003ePathloader and Guidloader are malware used to download and execute encrypted shellcode in memory. They have only been observed in association with Finaldraft.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ca href=\"https://unit42.paloaltonetworks.com/advanced-backdoor-squidoor/\"\u003ePalo Alto also published a report\u003c/a\u003e about the custom malware used by Jewelbug, though it dubbed the Finaldraft backdoor Squidoor. It also noted that Jewelbug gained access to networks of interest by exploiting various vulnerabilities in Internet Information Services (IIS) servers before deploying webshells on infected servers. It also said that as well as using the Microsoft Graph API for C\u0026amp;C communication, the attackers also used DNS and ICMP tunnelling.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003ePrevious reporting about Jewelbug has also noted the use of the CDB tool, the use of mspaint.exe to inject commands, and the wide-ranging use of dual-use and living off the land tools by the attackers as well, all of which was also seen in the recent activity observed by the Threat Hunter Team.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAll indications point to Jewelbug being of Chinese origin, with its motivation most likely to be espionage and maintaining a long-term and stealthy presence on compromised networks.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eChinese threat actors looking in a new direction?\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe most notable element of all this recent Jewelbug activity is the targeting of a Russian IT service provider by the Chinese APT group. When it comes to things like the Russia-Ukraine conflict and other geopolitical matters, China has traditionally backed, or at least not opposed, Russia, with the two considered to be loosely allied. The targeting of a Russian organization by a Chinese APT group shows, however, that Russia is not out-of-bounds when it comes to operations by China-based actors. The fact that there are indications the IT service provider may have been targeted for the purposes of a software supply chain attack on the company’s customers in Russia is also notable as it means this attack had the potential to give the attackers access to a large number of companies in the country, which they could have used for cyber espionage or disruption.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eJewelbug’s use of a new backdoor-in-development in this set of activity is also important as it shows that the group is continuing to actively develop its toolset and capabilities. Jewelbug, as a relatively new Chinese APT group, is one to watch as it has the skills to develop its own malware and maintain a long-term and stealthy presence on networks.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eIndicators of compromise (IOCs)\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFile 2\u0026gt;\u0026amp;1\u003c/p\u003e"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"17:T1dde,"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"\u003cp\u003eAttackers deploying the LockBit ransomware have continually evolved their tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs) to evade detection and maximize its impact. Among their sophisticated arsenal, two techniques stand out for their effectiveness in concealing malicious activities: DLL sideloading and masquerading. This blog post delves into how attackers deploying LockBit leverage these methods to establish persistence and blend into legitimate system processes.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile this blog details methods used in LockBit ransomware attacks specifically, similar methods are used by attackers deploying other malicious payloads as well.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eDLL sideloading: Exploiting trust for malicious gain\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eDLL sideloading is a stealthy attack technique where a legitimate application is tricked into loading a malicious Dynamic Link Library (DLL) instead of a legitimate one. This often occurs when a legitimate application attempts to load a DLL from an unsecured location or when the malicious DLL has the same name as a legitimate one but is placed in a directory that is searched earlier by the operating system.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eLockBit has been observed using DLL sideloading to execute its payload. Here's a simplified breakdown of the process:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLegitimate application as a loader:\u003c/strong\u003e LockBit often bundles its malicious DLL with a legitimate, digitally signed application. This application, when executed, will attempt to load specific DLLs required for its functionality.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMalicious DLL placement:\u003c/strong\u003e The LockBit threat actors place their malicious DLL (often with a filename identical to a legitimate one) in a directory that is searched by the legitimate application before the legitimate DLL's actual location.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eExecution of malicious code:\u003c/strong\u003e When the legitimate application is launched, it inadvertently loads and executes the malicious DLL, allowing the ransomware to initiate its encryption process or other nefarious activities under the guise of a trusted program.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis technique is particularly effective because it leverages the trust placed in legitimate applications, making it harder for traditional security solutions to differentiate between legitimate and malicious activity.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eMasquerading: Blending in with legitimate processes\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eMasquerading refers to the technique of disguising malicious files or processes to appear as legitimate system files or applications. LockBit actors employ various masquerading tactics to avoid detection and maintain persistence on compromised systems.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eCommon masquerading techniques observed in LockBit attacks include:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eRenaming malicious files:\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;Threat actors rename their ransomware executables or associated files to common system filenames (e.g., svchost.exe, explorer.exe, cmd.exe). This makes it difficult for users or even some security tools to distinguish them from legitimate system processes.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eSpoofing process names:\u003c/strong\u003e LockBit may also attempt to manipulate process names to appear as legitimate services or applications running on the system. This can be achieved by injecting code into legitimate processes or by using specific APIs to alter process information.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eUsing legitimate icons:\u003c/strong\u003e To further enhance their disguise, LockBit samples have been found to use icons identical to those of legitimate Windows applications, making the malicious files appear less suspicious to a casual observer.\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLeveraging legitimate directories:\u003c/strong\u003e\u0026nbsp;Malicious files are often placed in directories commonly used by legitimate system files, such as C:\\Windows\\System32 or C:\\ProgramData, further aiding in their camouflage.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn recent LockBit cases, attackers have been observed leveraging trusted applications alongside malicious DLLs (disguised with legitimate names) placed in the same directory. The technique relies on DLL side-loading, where the legitimate application inadvertently loads the malicious DLL. Examples include:\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eJarsigner.exe and jli.dll\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003eBoth are parts of the Java platform. Jarsigner.exe naturally loads jli.dll. In this case, the attacker placed a legitimate jarsigner.exe and a malicious jli.dll (disguised with the correct name) in the same folder. When jarsigner.exe was executed, it loaded the malicious jli.dll, which acted as a loader for further payload execution.\u003cbr\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eMpCmdRun.exe and mpclient.dll\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003eThese components belong to Windows Defender. The attacker dropped a renamed MpCmdRun.exe (masquerading as \u0026lt;company_domain_name\u0026gt;.exe) along with a malicious mpclient.dll. When \u0026lt;company_domain_name\u0026gt;.exe was run, it loaded mpclient.dll, which contained the LockBit ransomware payload and began encrypting files.\u003cbr\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eClink_x86.exe and clink_dll_x86.dll\u003c/strong\u003e\u003cbr\u003eThese are part of the Clink tool, which integrates with cmd.exe to enhance command-line input using its own Readline-based features. The attacker dropped a renamed Clink_x86.exe (again masquerading as \u0026lt;company_domain_name\u0026gt;.exe) alongside a malicious clink_dll_x86.dll. Upon execution, \u0026lt;company_domain_name\u0026gt;.exe loaded the malicious DLL clink_dll_x86.dll, triggering the LockBit ransomware payload and starting file encryption.\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp\u003eIn the following section, we’ll take a closer look into how the LockBit attacker is leveraging these techniques at various stages of the attack chain in recently seen attacks.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttack chain:\u0026nbsp;Initial access\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eA recent LockBit attack started with the attackers accessing machines using well-known remote desktop tools like MeshAgent, TeamViewer etc. Once an attacker gets access to remote machines, files are uploaded directly and executed.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttack chain: Privilege escalation\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eNSSM:\u003c/strong\u003e \u003ca href=\"https://nssm.cc/\"\u003eNSSM\u003c/a\u003e\u0026nbsp;was used to run a remote access Trojan (RAT) as a service. NSSM is a utility that can launch any normal application as a Windows service. NSSM created a service for the RAT, which it named edge.exe.exe, and started the service. We also saw this RAT renamed as o.exe.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePsExec:\u003c/strong\u003e PsExec was used to run cmd as a SYSTEM user using the “-s” parameter\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u003ccode\u003ePsExec64.exe -s -i cmd\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttack chain:\u0026nbsp;Discovery\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eNet.exe, nltest.exe and query.exe were used to get details about domain users, domains group, and group permissions.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003enet user \u0026lt;username\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003enet group \"domain computers\" /do\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003enltest /domain_trusts\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003equery user\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eAttack chain: Credential theft\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eTokenUtils.exe was used to steal tokens from all users and use those user tokens to launch any applications. In this case the attacker used the “NT AUTHORITY\\SYSTEM” user token to run whoami and cmd.exe.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003eTokenUtils.exe ListTokens\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003eTokenUtils.exe Execute -u “NT AUTHORITY\\SYSTEM” -e whoami -c\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003eTokenUtils.exe Execute -u “NT AUTHORITY\\SYSTEM” -e cmd.exe -c\u0026nbsp;\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eSd1.exe was also used to steal Kerberos tickets.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003eSd1 \u0026lt;sub-domain\u0026gt;.\u0026lt;domain\u0026gt;.local \u0026lt;domain\u0026gt;.local krbtgt\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp class=\"Code\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003eSd1 \u0026lt;sub-domain\u0026gt;.\u0026lt;domain\u0026gt;.local \u0026lt;domain\u0026gt; \u0026lt;domain\u0026gt;\\\u0026lt;domain user\u0026gt;\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"18:T11fc,"])</script><script>self.__next_f.push([1,"\u003ch2\u003eAttack chain: Lateral movement\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eGroup Policy was used to drop payloads on the machines and started executing files.
